<div id="review_1833418865" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1833418865" />
    <a title="Iset" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4973578-iset"><img alt="Iset" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563895104p2/4973578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1833418865?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iset" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iset" href="/user/show/4973578-iset">Iset</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345511294" href="/book/show/13531834-star-wars">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1833418865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17027324329285400525">This may be controversial to those who think that <i>Darth Plagueis</i> heralded a glorious bright spot in the latter day of the Extended Universe… but it’s not that great. True, it indeed raises itself above the morass of churned out, 200-pages-in-hardback, simply forgettable novels that were really plaguing the EU towards the end. <i>Darth Plagueis</i> is fresh and interesting and stands out enough that you won’t forget which one it was. It’s a genuinely decent read. It’s just not a great read.<br/><br/>After all the</span>
  <span id="freeText17027324329285400525" style="display:none">This may be controversial to those who think that <i>Darth Plagueis</i> heralded a glorious bright spot in the latter day of the Extended Universe… but it’s not that great. True, it indeed raises itself above the morass of churned out, 200-pages-in-hardback, simply forgettable novels that were really plaguing the EU towards the end. <i>Darth Plagueis</i> is fresh and interesting and stands out enough that you won’t forget which one it was. It’s a genuinely decent read. It’s just not a great read.<br /><br />After all these years, we still know so little about the Sith – at least compared to the Jedi. Believe it or not, it’s hard for people to knowingly embrace being evil all the time. It takes a pretty sustained effort. So are the Sith just fooling themselves into thinking they’re in the right? If not, how do they overcome that pesky innate impulse to goodness? What are their techniques, their ways and rituals, their supposed knowledge and wisdom that strengthen their resolve and convince them that the Jedi have it wrong? Seriously, they must have these things otherwise what’s the point. This is the kind of thing I’ve idly wondered about for years, ever since becoming immersed in the world of <i>Star Wars</i>. It’s a critical collection of questions because the Jedi/Sith conflict, the dual nature of the light side and the dark side, defines the tale of Star Wars. Let me just say that after reading hundreds of book in the EU, I am none the wiser. Some characters say the Force has no ‘sides’, it’s how you use it. Other characters rubbish that and stick to the dark side/light side view. Some characters talk about succumbing to the dark side, other characters talk about controlling the Force itself as something almost tangible. Every Sith novel that comes out offers a tiny snippet of ritual but never actually answers the key questions definitively. And I suspect they never will. In that respect, <i>Darth Plagueis</i> offers nothing new.<br /><br />But still, I keep getting suckered into these things because dammit I want answers! Plus, the allure of finally discovering the deep dark history of a major character – Palpatine himself – was a huge lure. The novel does fairly well on that front. Everything is set up, everything is put into place and the events of pretty much everything that follows, chronologically, is explained and featured here. There’s even Armand Isard and Sate Pestage and Kinman Doriana. But that’s kind of the book’s weakness as well as its strength. It offers a pretty good overview of exactly what was going on behind the scenes and what the Grand Plan was, and it reads like a detailed history book of the events of the period. But it’s too good. Everything has been so meticulously retconned that it’s unbelievable. Jabba the Hutt? He was working for Plagueis. Gardulla the Hutt creating podracing events on Tatooine? Plagueis. Dooku’s leaving the Jedi Order? Plagueis. Sifo-Dyas placing the order for the clone army? Plagueis. And how convenient too that Plagueis should have been incredibly rich, and that his master, Tenebrous, should have been a starship designer. Come on now. Leave some stuff down to the forces of chaos and chance, Mr Luceno! Sometimes a smalltime Hutt gangster is just a smalltime Hutt gangster. As for Sifo-Dyas, I always just figured that was a really unimaginative alias for Sidious placing the order in disguise. You don’t need to overcomplicate it. You don’t need to have Plagueis come in and actually manipulate the Jedi Master over a period of decades to prompt the placing of the clone order. Just kill the Jedi and fake the order. Simple. But rather than follow simple logic, the book engages in making everything convoluted and interconnected, I guess to try and make Plagueis look like a master of his craft. This is boring though – the story lacks excitement with the outcome so assured. Bribing people to do whatever is required for the Sith Grand Plan? Not a problem. Pushing politicians about to do whatever he wants? Plagueis doesn’t even have to lift a finger. Heck, windfalls just keep falling into his lap like gifts from the Force… sorry, no, that’s wrong, I meant to say: Because the story needs them to in order for the movies to happen.<br /><br />I admit I am being harsh in my criticism. It’s a pretty interesting read if you read it as a history rather than an exciting tale. It fleshes out a lot of previously mysterious areas. It just does it a little too perfectly to be plausible. Case in point is Palpatine. The book dodges out of telling us his first name under some silly pretext that he has rejected his given name and simply wishes to be known forever by his family name. Urgh. The convenience is physically painful. And if you were looking for a fascinating insight into the mysteries of the Sith – look elsewhere. The novel touches on Plagueis’ experiments with midi-chlorians but it never delves into them in depth or leads us to any conclusions. Most of the book is taken up with the mundane and practical matters of executing the Grand Plan, with almost no insight into the Sith philosophy. The closest we get is chapters 12 and 13 in which snatches of Palpatine’s training outline some of Plagueis’ lessons or views regarding the Sith and the Force. I wish we’d had more of this stuff. And more emotion, to boot. Plagueis and Palpatine, for the most part go about executing their plan with the utmost cool and collectedness. Those that join them seem to have no qualms whatsoever. Yeah, I’ll work for the Sith. No biggie. This feels decidedly odd.<br /><br />I should explain that in the absence of actual solid answers about the Sith way, I have a hypothesis that much of what occurs in a Sith’s tale should be driven by high drama and emotion. Much of the draw towards these characters, for fans, is not so much the intimidation of them (which quickly wears off with familiarity), but the question of their motivations and just how it is they stick to the life they do. To make them truly scary and delve into that fascination of fans, I firmly believe that the lives of the Sith are chock full of high drama, emotion, and tragedy. Events spiralling out of control and their instinctive terrible reaction that drags the individual, kicking and screaming, into a place where they feel completely trapped, and thus prompted into perpetrating ever more dubious deeds in horrified desperation. Now that’s what makes for a great story! How scary, to be a good person caught up in the tides of seemingly unstoppable horror and tragedy, and what you might do to survive! How heart-wrenching and gripping to watch such a person make poor decision after poor decision, never realising that they had other options! This is always what made Palpatine such a compelling Emperor. The Palpatine of the original trilogy was a master manipulator and always knew with sickening accuracy exactly where to jab. He didn’t care about his own life if it meant the perpetuating of his evil. He made other people feel like they were caught up on that tide of their own desperation and fear and that they had no choices available to them. We learn a lot about Palpatine and about Darth Plagueis in this book, but every step they take down the path of the dark side is so calm and poised. Where is the horror and the fear? Where is the book’s dark heart?<br /><br /><b>6 out of 10</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="17027324329285400525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer938378426214266798"><b> 3.5 / 5 </b><br/><br/>I mostly enjoyed this SW novel, which acts as a prequel to the Prequel films. <br/><br/>Darth Plagueis first comes across as a “cool” Sith Lord, outwitting everyone like a Shakespearian villain, only to then succumb to his crazed desire for immortality. Even though Palpatine / Sidious plays a more central role halfway through, his inclusion (along with the wide array of side characters) makes the novel varied and gripping. <br/><br/>I’m probably in the minority saying this, but if you thought the political</span>
  <span id="freeText938378426214266798" style="display:none"><b> 3.5 / 5 </b><br /><br />I mostly enjoyed this SW novel, which acts as a prequel to the Prequel films. <br /><br />Darth Plagueis first comes across as a “cool” Sith Lord, outwitting everyone like a Shakespearian villain, only to then succumb to his crazed desire for immortality. Even though Palpatine / Sidious plays a more central role halfway through, his inclusion (along with the wide array of side characters) makes the novel varied and gripping. <br /><br />I’m probably in the minority saying this, but if you thought the political intrigue that permeated the Prequel films was entertaining then this book won’t disappoint.<br /><br />James Luceno’s world-building is detailed while still leaving room for the reader’s imagination. The dialogues are fun too, and very much reminiscent of the ones in the Prequels.</span>

<div id="review_1540487927" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1540487927" />
    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1256262-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442983573p2/1256262.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1540487927?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/1256262-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256262-ron?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1256262-ron?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1540487927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6526198611131190925">“No true Sith cares about another.”<br/><br/>A worthy addition to the Expanded Universe. No, not four stars on any absolute scale of literary merit. (Is such a thing possible?) But certainly better conceived and executed than most Star Wars EU books. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11099729.Star_Wars_Darth_Plagueis' title='Star Wars Darth Plagueis by James Luceno' rel='nofollow noopener'>Star Wars: Darth Plagueis</a> joins many threads from the EU into the canon of Star War orthodoxy (the movies).<br/><br/>“We stalk the Force like hunters, rather than surrender like prey to its enigmatic whims.”<br/><br/>Don’t let the title fool you, this book is mostly about Palpat</span>
  <span id="freeText6526198611131190925" style="display:none">“No true Sith cares about another.”<br /><br />A worthy addition to the Expanded Universe. No, not four stars on any absolute scale of literary merit. (Is such a thing possible?) But certainly better conceived and executed than most Star Wars EU books. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11099729.Star_Wars_Darth_Plagueis" title="Star Wars Darth Plagueis by James Luceno" rel="nofollow noopener">Star Wars: Darth Plagueis</a> joins many threads from the EU into the canon of Star War orthodoxy (the movies).<br /><br />“We stalk the Force like hunters, rather than surrender like prey to its enigmatic whims.”<br /><br />Don’t let the title fool you, this book is mostly about Palpatine AKA Darth Sidious. Oh, it details the genesis, rise and reign of Hego Damask as Plagueis the Wise, but mostly the story is about how Palpatine was apprenticed by and superseded his Sith master. No, mostly the book reiterates copious Star Wars trivia, tying as many loose ends as possible from the EU into the canonical fourth movie (Episode One).<br /><br />“The power of the dark side is an illness no true Sith would wish to be cured of.”<br /><br />One of those threads is the theology of the Force. From Obi Wan Kenobi’s opening synopsis of Force theology in Episode Four, the Force has grown from, “The Force is … an energy field created by all living things. It surrounds us, and penetrates us. It binds the galaxy together,” to an independent consciousness able to will and choose light and dark. “The will of the Force begets …” and “[The Force] will strike back.” Therefore, differences of opinion as to the nature and use of the force naturally become sectarian warfare. (Think how Christians and Moslems settle their different concepts of the nature of God.) To themselves Sith are not evil, they’re just following a more real (and powerful) reality.<br /><br />“Sith are more powerful than Jedi because we are not afraid to feel.”<br /><br />Darth Plagueis is a space opera with very little space, let alone lightsaber fights, space battles, etc. Lots of dialogue and narrative. Reads like a history book—one on those which interprets everything “from a certain point of view.” With some ridiculous social commentary: “I blame the Senate for encouraging the galaxy to turn on credit.” Or “In the absence of armies there can be no wars.” Star Wars fans will give it a five, the rest may award three stars.<br /><br />“What celestial body is more luminous than a singularity, hiding in plain sight but more powerful than all?”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6526198611131190925" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1540487927">

<div id="review_269725724" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/269725724" />
    <a title="George Trello" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7365151-george-trello"><img alt="George Trello" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356032916p2/7365151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/269725724?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George Trello" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George Trello" href="/user/show/7365151-george-trello">George Trello</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer269725724&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer269725724" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5650609631336594213">I've read a lot of Star Wars books (though, I've apparently not added many to my shelves on here) and they typically, like the movies, focus more on the actions of characters rather than their motives. This one is different. There is very little action here; in fact it reads almost like a historical account of the rise and fall of Darth Plagueis as told by his apprentice, Darth Sidious (known better by those unfamiliar with the SW:EU as his alter ego Emperor Palpatine). It deals heavily with the</span>
  <span id="freeText5650609631336594213" style="display:none">I've read a lot of Star Wars books (though, I've apparently not added many to my shelves on here) and they typically, like the movies, focus more on the actions of characters rather than their motives. This one is different. There is very little action here; in fact it reads almost like a historical account of the rise and fall of Darth Plagueis as told by his apprentice, Darth Sidious (known better by those unfamiliar with the SW:EU as his alter ego Emperor Palpatine). It deals heavily with the one subject I found to be the saving grace of the dreaded prequels so many people seem to despise: politics. We watch as Plagueis, through his alter ego Hego Damask, uses his significant wealth as a high ranking member of the InterGalactic Banking Clan to fund the advancements of the Sith plot to rule while his newly found apprentice Palpatine, now Sidious, uses his gift of wit to advance politically by taking advantage of the situations they mutually create. That is, before Plageuis isolates himself and leaves all the scheming to his apprentice (and Palpatine's trained Sith assassin, Maul) so that he may continue his deep study of manipulating the Force in order to live forever. I found it marvelously interesting to learn about the history of these two descendants of Bane who organized the final steps to achieving the Sith's ultimate revenge: the downfall of the Jedi. I now feel that the tale of the Galactic Civil War is complete.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5650609631336594213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/292247357" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4131838-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1414009631p2/4131838.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/292247357?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer12175486722380886289">James Luceno weaves a tale of the Dark Side of the Force from 67 years before the Battle of Yavin through the Battle of Naboo.<br/><br/>To be completely candid, only fairly serious fans of Star Wars will "get this" - everyone else won't be very successful in making sense of what's going on.<br/><br/>I won't give away any books spoilers, but what I will say is that if you've wondered about the origins of Senator Palpatine/Darth Sidious, Darth Maul, Count Dooku, Nute Gunray, and Jedi Master Sifo-Dyas, as well as how </span>
  <span id="freeText12175486722380886289" style="display:none">James Luceno weaves a tale of the Dark Side of the Force from 67 years before the Battle of Yavin through the Battle of Naboo.<br /><br />To be completely candid, only fairly serious fans of Star Wars will "get this" - everyone else won't be very successful in making sense of what's going on.<br /><br />I won't give away any books spoilers, but what I will say is that if you've wondered about the origins of Senator Palpatine/Darth Sidious, Darth Maul, Count Dooku, Nute Gunray, and Jedi Master Sifo-Dyas, as well as how Amidala became Queen of the Naboo, and why there are always only two Sith Lords, then this is the book for you. An alternative title for the book could actually be "Star Wars: Darth Plageuis (Episode 0).<br /><br />The book sort of dragged for me at first, but once it started to introduced characters I was more familiar with things picked up and rolled right along.<br /><br />All in all I'd say that I'm glad I read the book...it provided interesting insights into the preliminaries leading up to the Battle of Naboo and the emergence of Darth Sidious, Darth Maul, and hints of Darth Vader.<br /><br />4 stars</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11196659630923299653">"Star Wars: Darth Plagueis" for me is the biggest event in the saga since the release of Episode III. After years of waiting, this novel finally reveals the origins of the legendary Emperor Palpatine, as well as the legend of his master, Darth Plagueis the Wise. More important, it does it well. Palpatine is as evil as a man can</span>
  <span id="freeText11196659630923299653" style="display:none">"Star Wars: Darth Plagueis" for me is the biggest event in the saga since the release of Episode III. After years of waiting, this novel finally reveals the origins of the legendary Emperor Palpatine, as well as the legend of his master, Darth Plagueis the Wise. More important, it does it well. I know that many fans dislike the truth behind the mask of Darth Vader - that he once was a lovesick emotional teenager. I am not among them, but I can tell them - relax. Palpatine is as evil as a man can be, and then some. Same can be said about his master, who had a much larger role in the success of his apprentice that you may suspect. The arrogance and the power of the Sith will astonish you and their cunning will shock you. Here and there, you see nods for the fans of the Expanded Universe, which a true Star Wars fan will treasure. In the end "Darth Plaguies" is a feast for all who love the story, which happened a long time ago, in a galaxy far, far away...</span>

  <span id="freeText10160856260547046677" style="display:none">Ahh, Darth Plagueis the Wise 0.00000001%er...Your book was so much more interesting over the first two thirds when the author did not have to flex and contort all plausibility in service of the bat guano crazy plotting of Dear Uncle George's prequels trilogy.<br /><br />Plagueis himself made for a very intriguing, deep-scheming villain- merciless and inhumane, yes, but also intelligent and subtle. I would've been perfectly satisfied if the book had ended with the completion of his "seduction" of young Palpatine to the dark side and the ways of the Sith, but plodding on through literally decades of double lives, senatorial intrigue, and endless cameos by familiar figures spoiled the final act for me.<br /><br />Still, Luceno is a heck of a writer, and listening to Daniel Davis' narration was a real treat- the man's got some golden pipes, to be sure, though he might need some help with his Huttese.</span>

<div id="review_1713300149" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1713300149" />
    <a title="Lance Shadow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/46445602-lance-shadow"><img alt="Lance Shadow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1474240407p2/46445602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1713300149?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lance Shadow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lance Shadow" href="/user/show/46445602-lance-shadow">Lance Shadow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46445602-lance-shadow?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46445602-lance-shadow?shelf=legends">legends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46445602-lance-shadow?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1713300149&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1713300149" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11544276155662952599">This is probably the hardest book review I have ever done. James Luceno is a prime example of an author that for me, I can tell he is very good at what he does, and yet I still have an extremely hard time with his books. The rating here was VERY hard to set, and it's likely bloated. On the one hand, at least half of this book left me quite bored, yet on the other I can understand why other people loved it. I will say, though, I listened to the audiobook narrated by Daniel Davis, and the audio ve</span>
  <span id="freeText11544276155662952599" style="display:none">This is probably the hardest book review I have ever done. James Luceno is a prime example of an author that for me, I can tell he is very good at what he does, and yet I still have an extremely hard time with his books. The rating here was VERY hard to set, and it's likely bloated. On the one hand, at least half of this book left me quite bored, yet on the other I can understand why other people loved it. I will say, though, I listened to the audiobook narrated by Daniel Davis, and the audio version in particular was awesome. <br /><br />THE STORY: This book is basically the life of Darth Plagueis. After taking over the mantle of the sith, Plagueis sets out on a journey to understand the force in order to bend it to his will and become immortal. In the mean time, he uses his alias Higo Damask along with his company, Damask Holdings, to amass wealth and power. <br />It also fleshes out Palpatine by giving him an origin story. It shows how he came under the tutelage of Plagueis and took the name Sidious, it shows his training under Plagueis as well as his gradual and steady rise in politics. <br />The book also shows how Darth Maul and Count Dooku fit in with everything by going into Sidious discovering and training Maul, and by setting up how Dooku will eventually join sidious as the replacement for Maul. Through this story about Plagueis, and to a lesser extent Sidious, Luceno ties The Phantom Menace with the Darth Bane Trilogy, effectively forming the final connective link between the Old Republic era of the EU with the Prequels and beyon. He even throws in a reference to Jorus C'Baoth of Timothy Zahn's Thrawn Trilogy (thank you Daniel Davis for finally teaching me how to pronounce that character's name! :D) and a really vague prophecy that either foreshadows the Clone Wars, the Yuuzhan Vong, or both. <br /><br />THE BAD: Like I said before, Plagueis, as well written as it was, left me quite bored for large portions of the book. <br />The way Luceno writes often does not work for me. I really want to love is work but it always leaves me bored for one reason or another. With <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22012250.Tarkin__Star_Wars_" title="Tarkin (Star Wars) by James Luceno" rel="nofollow noopener">Tarkin</a>, it was obsessive info dumping on objects or characters that only appeared once and didn't impact anything in the book. Here, it's the overwhelming number of boring scenes where characters just do nothing but talk about business deals and politics. Seriously someone swing a lightsaber or just shoot something! I know other people found these scenes interesting but for me, the constant barrage of scenes that just had people talking got frustrating. This was especially a problem when the book focused on Palpatine, as I didn't find a single political scene that focused on him at all interesting. However, the scenes where Plagueis was talking to business leaders negotiating deals was not that interesting either. <br /><br />THE GOOD: I can totally see why other people loved this book. As bored as I was with alot of it, the stuff that is good is extremely good. <br />Let's start with the characters. Yeah the side characters weren't very interesting, but the main characters more than made up for it.<br />Darth Plagueis is absolutely fantastic in this book. This is what Darth Bane in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35430.Path_of_Destruction__Star_Wars_Darth_Bane___1_" title="Path of Destruction (Star Wars Darth Bane, #1) by Drew Karpyshyn" rel="nofollow noopener">Path of Destruction</a> SHOULD HAVE BEEN. Plagueis is an excellently realized villain, and he is one of the best characters the Legends continuity has to offer. Yes he's wealthy and powerful in the dark side, but outside the boring business talks I love how this character was written. He's cunning. He's devious. He's brilliant. This is everything that makes a terrifying evildoer. I started reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/216443.Heir_to_the_Empire__Star_Wars_The_Thrawn_Trilogy___1_" title="Heir to the Empire (Star Wars The Thrawn Trilogy, #1) by Timothy Zahn" rel="nofollow noopener">Heir to the Empire</a> recently and even though I'm not even at the halfway point I already know that Thrawn is pretty awesome. But honestly, I don't think he will be able to top Plagueis. His dialogue is great. His personality is great. His character arc is amazing. The way he tries to change the sith from a power focused pair into one that aims for galactic political power is really interesting, and I love his efforts to change and circumvent the Rule of Two established by Bane. I also liked all his experimentation with Venemous and the other life forms. <br />The last chapter of this book is great, and saved an important aspect of the book for me: Palpatine. Just about any scene where Palpatine was not with Plagueis was incredibly dull and boring because they were all political talks- the only exceptions were the scene where he first discovers Maul, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the scene where he kills his family  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and the scenes in the epilogue. But then the final chapter comes along. No spoilers, but man did he shine. All of the boring political talks were tied together in one amazing speech that brought out how devious and manipulative he is. It was totally reminiscent of Stover's Palpatine from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35458.Star_Wars_Episode_III___Revenge_of_the_Sith" title="Star Wars Episode III - Revenge of the Sith by Matthew Woodring Stover" rel="nofollow noopener">Revenge of the Sith</a> and it was great to listen to. <br />The scenes where Palpatine and Plagueis are together are mostly very good. There are a few scenes where they just talk about politics and business deals that are a bore, but any time that they discuss the force or the grand plan, it is really engrossing and interesting. I also really liked their interactions in the last chapter. One thing I noticed in James Luceno's work is how he always tend to write decent payoffs in his last chapters. <br />Luceno's efforts to tie in to the expanded universe are very well done. I really liked how he explained Vitiate from SWTOR and made light of his "immortality" by going into how Plagueis was trying to one up him. I liked the references to the Bane books and how Plagueis was trying to learn from them. I liked all the little bits of information that show how everything in the Phantom Menace got set up, as well as the foreshadowing for the Clone Wars. It was also really interesting to see how many ideas were pulled from this book and added to the CGI Clone Wars series from 2008, particularly the lore surrounding Darth Maul (Dathomir/Nightsisters/Mother Talzin), but also characters like Orn Free Taa and Onaconda Farr. <br />If there's one detail that I'm highly impressed with, it's how James Luceno made sense of the midichlorians. To this day, people consider midichlorians up there with Jar Jar Binks as one of the biggest stains ever to hit the Star Wars Universe. And even after reading this book, yeah, I still think the midichlorians are dumb. But I can't fault Luceno for incorporating them into his novel, they weren't his fault. He saw them as part of the universe, good or bad, and wanted to make sense of it. I think he did a fantastic job making them fit a little bit more naturally in the universe of Star Wars and he was able to have the midichlorians and yet still give the classic sense of mysticism to the force. It also helps that Luceno did a great job integrating them as part of Plagueis's plan to achieve immortality through and mastery over the force. <br />Finally, while I did find most of the talking scenes boring, I can understand why other readers find them interesting. Unlike Luceno's tendency to info dump that was apparent in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22012250.Tarkin__Star_Wars_" title="Tarkin (Star Wars) by James Luceno" rel="nofollow noopener">Tarkin</a>, none of these scenes felt pointless. They all served a purpose and helped advance the main characters' plans to take over the Republic. <br /><br />THE AUDIO- OR AS I LIKE TO CALL IT, THE AWESOME: This is the best voice work I have seen from a Star Wars audio book. Sure Marc Thompson is great and overall I would prefer him to Daniel Davis for most Star Wars books. Thompson's voice is just a natural fit for Star Wars narration. However, I could not see anyone else besides Daniel Davis narrating Plagueis. This voice performance was beyond pitch perfect, and it elevates this novel by leaps and bounds. <br />His voice for Plagueis is unbelievably awesome. It is cold, ruthless, and menacing. The octave Davis uses and the way he delivers Plageuis's lines is absolutely spine- chilling.<br />I know it's weird to focus on, but I really liked the voice he used for 114D, Plagueis's droid servant. Davis elevated this droid from an otherwise forgettable side character that just combines the utility of R2D2 with C3PO's polished and polite dialogue into a pretty interesting little companion for Plagueis. I love how just through the voice work 114D becomes a clever little foil to the manipulative, calculating Plagueis as an upbeat, unquestionably loyal sidekick of sorts.<br />The other voices are great too. Palpatine is totally spot on and Davis' voice for palpatine works wonders in immersion, and Maul has a cool voice as well. <br />I got to say, as bored as I was for some of those portions, Daniel Davis' voice got me through it. He made it sound good even if I wasn't invested, so I was totally ok with just listening to Davis' voice as he read down the page. <br /><br />THE VERDICT: Darth Plagueis, despite the EU wipe, still holds up pretty well because almost none of it has been retconned by the canon and alot of its lore was incorporated into canon through works like The Clone Wars. In that respect, it can still serve as the final piece of the puzzle that started with the Darth Bane Trilogy and bridged the gap between the KOTOR/SWTOR era and The Phantom Menace, which remains as the earliest story chronologically in the new canon. <br />I went in worried that I might not like this novel, but I was happy that that wasn't the case. Is it one of my favorities? no. Did I still like it? yes. <br /> James Luceno's Darth Plagueis is strongly written with excellent characterization and well integrated tie ins that connect the the Prequels with the Expanded Universe. I will admit though, I would have liked it far less if I was just reading the book and not listening to the audio version from Daniel Davis. However, I also confess that I'm not really the kind of person who is grabbed by Luceno's writing. I'm fine with the fact that it seems like he prefers to focus more on politics and galactic trade than the jedi or sith, but sometimes he can be a bit overzealous with that focus. A little bit of politics or business dealings can be great to flesh out the Star Wars universe, but Luceno's excessive use of it in this book really dragged on me. <br />I had trouble getting through this book sometimes, but the stuff that is good is really freaking good. In the end, though I'm probably not the first to admit this, Plagueis is NOT a book for everybody. Some people who read this book are going to love it and think that it is absolutely brilliant. They will be enthralled by how all the politics advances the plans of the two main characters as well as tie everything together. Others will bemoan the lack of action and the lack of focus on the force, and will find it excruciatingly boring and hate it. I can fully understand both perspectives, and even though I'm more on the side of liking this book, I acknowledge that I personally struggled with it sometimes. <br />I don't think just being interested in the story of Darth Plagueis is enough to recommend this book. Plagueis' character and story arc is fantastic in this novel, but it doesn't carry the book completely. You will also really need to be interested in learning more about Palpatine and his political career as well as how the expanded universe connects with the films, particularly the prequels and especially The Phantom Menace. <br />If you want to know all about that extra stuff, great, this book will work for you. Otherwise, I'd skip it because you'll frequently get bored. <br />However, I will say that if you're interested in reading this or even just on the fence, please listen to the audiobook. The force is with this book, but Daniel Davis really makes you feel the power of the dark side with his incredible voice performance. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11544276155662952599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_430535777" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/430535777" />
    <a title="Jeff Diamond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2591881-jeff-diamond"><img alt="Jeff Diamond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1460780749p2/2591881.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/430535777?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff Diamond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff Diamond" href="/user/show/2591881-jeff-diamond">Jeff Diamond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780307969248" href="/book/show/13135235-darth-plagueis">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2591881-jeff-diamond?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2591881-jeff-diamond?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer430535777" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17054594407326875623"><i>Darth Plagueis was a Dark Lord of the Sith who was so wise and so powerful that he could influence the midichloreans to create life. He had such a knowledge of the Dark Side that he could even keep the ones he cared about from dying.</i><br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11099729.Star_Wars_Darth_Plagueis' title='Star Wars Darth Plagueis by James Luceno' rel='nofollow noopener'>Star Wars: Darth Plagueis</a> is one of the best Star Wars books that I've read. Period. I could stop writing now, and that's all you'd need to know. But since it's me, I'll go a little deeper.<br/><br/>Darth Plagueis follows the title character and his nefarious dealings througho</span>
  <span id="freeText17054594407326875623" style="display:none"><i>Darth Plagueis was a Dark Lord of the Sith who was so wise and so powerful that he could influence the midichloreans to create life. He had such a knowledge of the Dark Side that he could even keep the ones he cared about from dying.</i><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11099729.Star_Wars_Darth_Plagueis" title="Star Wars Darth Plagueis by James Luceno" rel="nofollow noopener">Star Wars: Darth Plagueis</a> is one of the best Star Wars books that I've read. Period. I could stop writing now, and that's all you'd need to know. But since it's me, I'll go a little deeper.<br /><br />Darth Plagueis follows the title character and his nefarious dealings throughout the galaxy. Many things are revealed to us, which seems fitting given what little we had heard of Plagueis before. As with other books in the Star Wars universe, this one is teeming with life that we both know and that which we don't know. Many of the events and undercurrents are explained in the book, and we can see just how much power and influence each of the characters had on the galaxy as a whole. Aside from how it ties in, there doesn't seem to be any action, which is unusual in the Star Wars franchise. Most of the compelling action is centered around character, money, power and politics. Essentially, it succeeded where Episodes I and II failed. So much so, in fact, that the two films may be tolerable after reading Darth Plagueis.<br /><br />Writing is solid, and almost terrifying. The characters come to life, and the characters in this book are true villains. Not necessarily the type of villain we get in 1920s melodrama, but the kind of bad guy that knows he's in the right and will do anything or destroy anyone who tries to stop him. With such cold and calculated moves, we are sucked into the book and it keeps us going with a kind of morbid curiousity about how they are going to go about their evil deeds. Although it brings us to a kind of fearful state, it is well-written. Between the fear and the great prose, we are left in a kind of fearful reverie. At once enjoying sublime writing about some of the darkest deeds ever done in the years leading up to the Clone Wars. I'm already wondering when I'll have time to read it again.<br /><br />The one complaint that I could possibly imagine about this book is the depth of the theory in regards to the Force. It gets into the deep theories about how the Force works, and I had to back up and go over some portions three or four times. In the end, I just kind of assumed that it was something that I hadn't read about in the Expanded Universe yet. If you've read a lot of the EU, then you won't have a problem. If you're still fairly new to Star Wars books, just realize that you may not understand everything that they're saying because...You've taken your fist step into a larger world. <br /><br />I came away from this book thinking of one word: wow. I am impressed at how well done this book was in every aspect. Whether or not you like the Prequel Trilogy, Darth Plagueis is certainly worth a read (or listen), and is so good that it may even make the first two films more enjoyable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17054594407326875623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_273181541" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/273181541" />
    <a title="Quinn Rollins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6677104-quinn-rollins"><img alt="Quinn Rollins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439782464p2/6677104.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/273181541?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quinn Rollins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quinn Rollins" href="/user/show/6677104-quinn-rollins">Quinn Rollins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6677104-quinn-rollins?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6677104-quinn-rollins?shelf=biography">biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer273181541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3379208742033920102">Toward the middle of Star Wars Episode III: Revenge of the Sith, Chancellor Palpatine and Anakin Skywalker attend the opera. Evidently “opera” in Star Wars involves giant soap bubbles and extended bass notes. As they’re sitting in Palpatine’s box seats, the old man tells Anakin a tale about Darth Plagueis. It’s a bit of bait to nudge Anakin closer to the dark side, with Palpatine telling him that Plagueis found a way to extend life and avoid death. Since this loss is what Anakin fears most, it’s</span>
  <span id="freeText3379208742033920102" style="display:none">Toward the middle of Star Wars Episode III: Revenge of the Sith, Chancellor Palpatine and Anakin Skywalker attend the opera. Evidently “opera” in Star Wars involves giant soap bubbles and extended bass notes. As they’re sitting in Palpatine’s box seats, the old man tells Anakin a tale about Darth Plagueis. It’s a bit of bait to nudge Anakin closer to the dark side, with Palpatine telling him that Plagueis found a way to extend life and avoid death. Since this loss is what Anakin fears most, it’s just the thing to reel him in. It’s a bizarre interlude in the movie, and that’s all we knew of Darth Plagueis…until now. <br /><br />James Luceno has written some of my favorite recent Star Wars novels, including Millennium Falcon, Cloak of Deception, and Dark Lord: The Rise of Darth Vader. He understands the Star Wars universe and the connections between movies and trilogies, but he’s also a gifted writer, with a knack for the voices and motivations each character has. His most recent novel is Star Wars: Darth Plagueis. <br /><br />This book is a dual biography of a Sith Master and Apprentice: Darth Plagueis and Darth Sidious. Darth Plagueis is an alien known as a muun in the Star Wars universe—spindly humanoids with heads that look like they’ve been squeezed in a vice. They run the Banking Clan, and Magister Hego Damask has incredible wealth and power at his disposal. He becomes a Sith Lord, and gains more power and more experience, until he feels capable of destroying his own master—which he does in the first chapters of the novel. At that point, Damask/Darth Plagueis becomes the master, and needs an apprentice. Palpatine is a spoiled rich teenager on Naboo, already interested in politics and frustrated that his ambitions exceed his grasp. As Palpatine and Damask’s paths cross, the latter senses the strength of the Force in the former, and begins to tutor him in the ways of the Sith. <br /><br />While there’s plenty of Star Wars-styled action, there are also a lot of political details, which I know are a turnoff for some. I liked the scenes set in the senate, and the manipulations of both Palpatine/Sidious and Damask/Plagueis to seat Palpatine as the Supreme Chancellor. They’re both power hungry, and they’re eventually sated. <br /><br />Where this book is essentially a prequel to the prequel trilogy, I worried I wouldn’t like it. I shouldn’t have been concerned. Luceno uses the uncharted territory of Palpatine’s life pre-Phantom Menace to spin an excellent story that has more surprises and twists in it than I expected. There are also more characters I recognized than I expected, including Jabba the Hutt, Count Dooku, Darth Maul, and others. It explains the Sith Order better and more concisely than other books I’ve read, and as the book continues, truly does become a tragedy worthy of the name. This was an excellent entry in the series, and if you’re a fan of Star Wars, is well worth your time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3379208742033920102" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_267645190" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/267645190" />
    <a title="Brad Wheeler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4959290-brad-wheeler"><img alt="Brad Wheeler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298932053p2/4959290.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/267645190?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad Wheeler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad Wheeler" href="/user/show/4959290-brad-wheeler">Brad Wheeler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4959290-brad-wheeler?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4959290-brad-wheeler?shelf=star-wars">star-wars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer267645190" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13800622005846535294">Throughout the extremely uneven history of the Star Wars Expanded Universe, certain authors have occasionally taken it upon themselves to write novels that are, at least in part, lengthy retcons of areas of the canon that have become confused or conflicting (e.g. <i>I, Jedi</i>, or <i>The Death Star</i>). <i>Darth Plagueis</i> does a fair bit of retconning, but beyond that, it's a Star Wars novel that's actually good as a <i>novel</i>, not just a Star Wars story.<br/><br/>The entire novel is told through the viewpoint of the eponymo</span>
  <span id="freeText13800622005846535294" style="display:none">Throughout the extremely uneven history of the Star Wars Expanded Universe, certain authors have occasionally taken it upon themselves to write novels that are, at least in part, lengthy retcons of areas of the canon that have become confused or conflicting (e.g. <i>I, Jedi</i>, or <i>The Death Star</i>). <i>Darth Plagueis</i> does a fair bit of retconning, but beyond that, it's a Star Wars novel that's actually good as a <i>novel</i>, not just a Star Wars story.<br /><br />The entire novel is told through the viewpoint of the eponymous Darth Plagueis or his apprentice, Palpatine. The way that Palpatine's family and background are handled make the man's later decisions seem entirely reasonable. Plagueis, for his part, is a wealthy and powerful individual, so he spends much the story manipulating Galactic events to his own ends. In the end, the Naboo crisis featured in Episode 1 forms the background to the book's climactic scenes, and it adds a lot of interesting context to that story. It also makes reference to the events of other Republic-era stories; I'm sure I missed half the references.<br /><br />At this point in the game, I'm thrilled with any Star Wars novels that don't drop the ball completely. I want to love the Expanded Universe, but most people don't treat it with the respect it deserves. <i>Darth Plagueis</i> does. It's well thought out, well constructed, and well written. A thoroughly entertaining story that I suspect will stand among the best Star Wars novels ever written.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13800622005846535294" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1462565269" />
    <a title="Kkeeleart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31849213-kkeeleart"><img alt="Kkeeleart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424451393p2/31849213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1462565269?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kkeeleart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kkeeleart" href="/user/show/31849213-kkeeleart">Kkeeleart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1462565269" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10464218262976067575">The problem is I read the premise, looked at the cover, and watched the scene from Revenge of the Sith where Palpatine tells the story of Darth Plagueis and I had a different story in my head than what this turned out to be. I'd imagined this book following Darth Plagueis as he traveled to ancient Sith ruins and played 'Frankenstein' on life forms and researched old dark side texts to uncover lost secrets of the force. There's some of that, but what we mostly get instead is a book about the orig</span>
  <span id="freeText10464218262976067575" style="display:none">The problem is I read the premise, looked at the cover, and watched the scene from Revenge of the Sith where Palpatine tells the story of Darth Plagueis and I had a different story in my head than what this turned out to be. I'd imagined this book following Darth Plagueis as he traveled to ancient Sith ruins and played 'Frankenstein' on life forms and researched old dark side texts to uncover lost secrets of the force. There's some of that, but what we mostly get instead is a book about the origins of Darth Sidious and all the political positioning behind the scenes to set him up as the Supreme Chancellor and set in motion the stuff that happens in episodes 1-3. It fills us in on a few details that those movies never explain, but since I have little affection for those stories I never really enjoyed it above the three stars I've rated it as. I liked it, it is well written and I'm impressed by how interesting the story remained despite the main characters all being bad guys who you're not necessarily interested in seeing succeed. Your enjoyment likely depends on your enjoyment of the prequels. I've seen reviews that say that reading this book improves those films but I did not find this to be case.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10464218262976067575" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sR/y8upbSFlMIS86+p7pB4Wdu7WVRbJoMuI8v0/Cg3Z9sqdii1tFm/dFURKUGF5AimRSsfQV/bkcpY9Ba5fDUw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }
